[Davyd Whaley - Wikipedia]
Davyd Whaley was born in Bristol, Tennessee, in 1967. He was a Los Angeles-based painter. He was a United States Navy Veteran of four years. Previous to his career as a painter, he was an electrical engineer for twenty years. He met television director Norman Buckley in 2004, and they were married (as soon as they were legally able) from 2008 until Whaley's death on October 15, 2014, in Los ...

[PDF Davyd Whaley]
Tennessee-born artist Davyd Whaley creates bold, colorful images that are at once narrative and abstract. Each work is loose and sensual, as aspects of human story and emotion unfold amid broad strokes of paint.

--- [Davyd Whaley - Wikipedia] ---
Davyd Whaley (December 6, 1967 – October 15, 2014) was an American abstract painter known for expressing in his paintings Jungian themes related to the world of the subconscious.

--- [Grokipedia: Davyd Whaley] ---
Davyd Whaley (December 6, 1967 – October 15, 2014) was an American abstract painter renowned for his large-scale canvases that delved into Jungian psychology, the subconscious mind, dreams, and natural forms through asymmetrical compositions and textured layering.[1][2]Born in the Appalachian mountains of Tennessee, Whaley overcame a challenging childhood marked by a severe stammer, turning to art as a form of therapy from age 12.[3] After serving four years in the U.S. Navy from 1986 to 1990 and earning a degree in electrical engineering from North Carolina State University, he worked for two decades in corporate engineering until a health crisis involving seizures prompted a career pivot to full-time painting around 2005.[3][2] Primarily self-taught, he honed his skills through studies at UCLA from 2012 to 2014 and earlier classes at the Art Students League of New York with mentors Ronnie Landfield and Larry Poons from 2008 to 2012.[2][1]Whaley's artistic process involved meticulous, episodic sequences on expansive canvases, often taking up to seven months per piece, employing a small filbert brush to build circuitous strokes, thick textures, and contrasts of light and color that evoked tactile depth and spiritual resonance.[3][1] As a resident artist at the Santa Fe Art Colony in downtown Los Angeles and a member of the Los Angeles Art Association, he exhibited widely, including solo shows like Habitual at Gallery 825 in 2014 and at Galerie Michael in Beverly Hills, as well as international venues such as the Florence Biennale in 2013 and Aqua Art Miami in 2013.[2][3] His commitment to community extended to teachingart to terminally ill and war-scarred children, earning him the Los Angeles City Council’s 2012 Volunteer of the Year award in Public Arts Education.[2][3]Following his death at age 46, Whaley's legacy endures through the Davyd Whaley Foundation, established in 2016 to provide grants and residencies to under-recognized and mid-career artists in Los Angeles, having distributed over $42,000 in support by 2019 and continuing to award grants and scholarships thereafter, including the Davyd Whaley Memorial Scholarship for abstract painting at the Art Students League of New York.[4][1][5] His works are documented in the book DAVYD WHALEY, featuring an essay by Peter Clothier, with proceeds benefiting the foundation.[1] Early Life and Education Childhood in Appalachia Davyd Whaley was born on December 6, 1967, in Bristol, Tennessee, a city straddling the Appalachian region on the Tennessee-Virginia border.[6][1] Growing up in the rural, mountainous landscape of eastern Tennessee, Whaley experienced a childhood marked by poverty and isolation, where economic hardships limited opportunities for cultural or artistic engagement.[3] The Appalachian environment, described as inhospitable to the liberal arts, offered few resources for creative pursuits, fostering a sense of solitude that profoundly shaped his early years.[3]By age 12, Whaley struggled with a severe stammer that hindered communication, leading to his placement in a special class where art served as a therapeutic tool to express thoughts and overcome his speech challenges.[3] Encouraged by this experience, he began self-taught sketching, using drawing as a means to clarify ideas and connect with others in a resource-scarce setting devoid of formal art supplies or training.[3] These early efforts ignited a deep passion for art, despite the lack of familial or communal support for such ambitions in his impoverished surroundings.[7]As a boy, Whaley harbored dreams of becoming a professional artist, envisioning a life beyond the constraints of his Appalachian upbringing, though practical barriers delayed his pursuit until later.[7] In high school, encouragement from an art teacher further nurtured his sketching and painting, providing the first glimpses of structured artistic exploration amid ongoing personal and environmental challenges.[3] This formative period in Appalachia instilled a resilient drive that would define his eventual transition to a dedicated artistic career. Military Service and Engineering Studies Following his high school graduation, Davyd Whaley enlisted in the U.S. Navy in 1986, serving for four years until 1990 primarily aboard submarines.[3] This period exposed him to the demands of underwater operations, where he developed essential skills in precision engineering and operational discipline required for maintaining complex systems in confined, high-stakes environments.[3]After completing his naval service, Whaley pursued higher education at North Carolina State University, where he earned a bachelor's degree in electrical engineering.[3] These studies provided a rigorous technical foundation that complemented the practical expertise gained in the Navy.[3]Whaley's time in the Navy profoundly shaped his work ethic, instilling a strong sense of discipline, attention to detail, and methodical problem-solving that became hallmarks of his approach to professional challenges.[3] These attributes, honed through the Navy's structured regimen, emphasized reliability and precision under pressure, influencing his subsequent career pursuits.[3] Transition to Art Engineering Career After earning his electrical engineering degree from North Carolina State University in the early 1990s, Davyd Whaley embarked on a professional career in the field, initially focusing on technical roles in corporate environments.[3] Over approximately two decades in corporate engineering until around 2005, he advanced steadily, rising to executive positions at several Fortune 500 companies where he managed complex engineering projects and teams.[8] His work emphasized precision and problem-solving in electrical systems, building directly on the technical expertise developed during his U.S. Navy service on submarines.[3]Whaley's engineering tenure provided financial stability and professional success, yet it was marked by high stress associated with corporate demands and a type-A work ethic. By his late 30s, persistent unfulfilled creative impulses—rooted in his lifelong interest in drawing and painting—began to weigh heavily, though his demanding schedule left little room for artistic exploration.[3]Around age 38 in 2005, a sudden health crisis involving a series of unexplained seizures forced Whaley to retire from engineering, enabling him to transition fully to art as a profession and channel his creative urges into painting.[3] This pivotal shift, supported by prudent investments from his engineering earnings, marked the end of his corporate career and the beginning of his dedicated artistic pursuits.[7] Initial Artistic Pursuits Following a health crisis that prompted a reevaluation of his priorities, Davyd Whaley eventually relocated to Los Angeles to fully immerse himself in the vibrant art scene, leveraging the financial stability from his prior engineering career to support this transition.[1] This move marked a deliberate pivot from corporate life, allowing him to dedicate himself to painting amid the city's dynamic creative community.[1]Whaley was largely self-taught as an artist, pursuing informal education without obtaining a formal degree in the field. From 2007 to 2011, he enrolled in coursework at the Art Students League of New York, studying under mentors such as Ronnie Landfield and Larry Poons, which honed his approach to abstract expressionism.[9] Complementing this, he took occasional extension classes at UCLA with instructors including Max Malansky and Nick Brown, further refining his skills through practical engagement rather than structured academia.[10]Whaley's initial professional steps solidified his presence in Los Angeles' art ecosystem. He became a resident artist at the Santa Fe Art Colony in downtown Los Angeles, securing a spacious loft studio that served as a creative haven for his daily practice.[1] Starting in 2011, he began teaching art classes for the City of Los Angeles Department of Recreation and Parks, instructing seniors and underprivileged youth in acrylic techniques and fostering community engagement with the arts.[3] Artistic Practice Style and Techniques Davyd Whaley primarily worked in abstract forms, favoring oil on canvas as his medium to create asymmetrical compositions that emphasize imbalance for visual tension.[8] His paintings feature three-dimensional contrasts achieved through tactile depth, strategic use of light and shadow, and bold, vivid colors that provoke energy and power on the surface.[8][3]A key technique in Whaley's practice involved extensive layering, often using impasto applications with a palette knife to build thick pigment layers, resulting in dynamic, textured surfaces that evoke movement and forceful depth.[8] He complemented this with precise detailing via a tiny filbert brush, allowing for opulent color effects and labor-intensive buildup that enhanced the painting's vibrancy.[3] These methods created raised, multidimensional canvases where light interacts with the impasto to generate a sense of spatial illusion and emotional intensity.[8]Whaley's style evolved from self-taught experimentation, where he initially explored basic oil techniques on various supports like paper and masonite, to more mature works characterized by refined layering and asymmetrical boldness that exuded heightened vibrancy and expressive power.[11][8] This progression is evident in his shift toward larger, unstretched canvases worked on the floor, enabling fluid, unconscious layering processes that matured into cohesive, impactful abstracts.[3] Whaley's techniques subtly incorporated influences from Jungian psychology in their thematic application, using layered contrasts to suggest subconscious emergence.[3] Themes and Influences Davyd Whaley's artistic oeuvre is deeply rooted in Jungian psychology, particularly the exploration of the unconscious mind as a source of profound insight and creative expression. Influenced by Carl Jung's theories, Whaley drew upon concepts such as archetypes—universal symbols embedded in the collective unconscious—and the process of individuation, which involves integrating conscious and unconscious elements of the psyche to achieve wholeness. His work often delved into dreams as portals to the subconscious, viewing them as bursts of revelation akin to thunderstorms that illuminate hidden aspects of the self.[1] This engagement with Jungian ideas stemmed from Whaley's personal practice of maintaining dream journals and undergoing analysis with a Jungian therapist, allowing him to translate introspective visions into visual narratives that probe the psyche's depths.[10]A central theme in Whaley's paintings is the balance between light and dark, representing the duality of human experience—conscious rationality versus unconscious instinct, joy versus shadow. He incorporated archetypal symbols, such as those evoking renewal and transformation, to symbolize psychological growth and the alchemical process of turning base elements into enlightened states. These symbols were informed by philosophy, including Jung's emphasis on synchronicity and the collective unconscious, as well as broader art historical precedents like the symbolic abstractions of artists such as Wassily Kandinsky, whose spiritual geometries resonated with Whaley's quest to manifest an alternate universe where the subconscious becomes tangible reality. Personal introspection played a pivotal role, as Whaley used painting as a therapeutic medium to process inner conflicts and epiphanies, often reflecting on how dreams and symbols reveal life's underlying patterns.[1][3]Whaley's Appalachian roots in the mountains of eastern Tennessee profoundly shaped his thematic concerns, infusing his work with reflections on resilience, hardship, and the pursuit of transcendence amid adversity. Growing up in a region where economic struggles and limited opportunities dominated, he experienced art as an early form of therapy to overcome personal challenges, such as a childhood stammer, fostering a lifelong motif of personal growth through creative liberation. His transition from a practical engineering career—marked by military service and professional stability—to full-time artistry mirrored the American Dream as a narrative of reinvention, embodying themes of renewal after life-altering health crises like seizures and a coma that prompted his shift toward healing through expression. These experiences informed explorations of transformation, portraying the artist's journey from constrained roots to expansive self-realization as a universal archetype of human potential.[3][4] Professional Achievements Exhibitions Davyd Whaley's exhibition history reflects his rising prominence in the Los Angeles and international art scenes during the early 2010s, with solo shows emphasizing his abstract explorations of the subconscious and group presentations showcasing his integration into broader contemporary dialogues. His first notable solo exhibition, Anima Speaks, was held at Brooklyn Art Gym in New York, presenting a series of works that delved into archetypal imagery drawn from Jungian psychology.[8] This show marked an early milestone in his career, highlighting his transition from engineering to full-time artistry through vibrant, layered abstractions. Following this, Whaley mounted Flood the Subconscious as a solo exhibition at Studio 12 Gallery in Denver, Colorado, from June 19 to August 16, 2010, where he created an immersive visual narrative of dream-like sequences and symbolic floods representing the overflow of unconscious thoughts.[12]In parallel, Whaley participated in several key group exhibitions that expanded his visibility. At Galerie Michael in Beverly Hills, Los Angeles, he featured in the inaugural group show from February 23 to April 4, 2013, alongside other emerging artists, contributing pieces that echoed themes of inner revelation.[2] That same year, his painting Heaven and Hell was selected for the IX Florence Biennale in Italy, held from November 30 to December 8, where it stood among international works addressing spiritual and psychological duality.[3] Whaley's paintings were also exhibited at Art Aqua Miami from December 3 to 8, 2013, and the Los Angeles Art Show from January 23 to 27, 2013.[2] Additionally, in 2012, Whaley's paintings were exhibited at the opening of the Gallery for the People summer show in Los Angeles on June 28, an event hosted by Hollywood producer Harvey Weinstein that benefited The Art of Elysium and underscored his growing connections in the entertainment-adjacent art community; this event led to the discovery of his work by the gallery representing him.[3][13]Whaley's solo presence at Galerie Michael continued to evolve, culminating in the 2014 exhibition Subconscious Tendencies, a solo show from February 22 onward that further solidified his reputation for paintings infused with Jungian motifs of the psyche's depths.[10] He also held a solo exhibitionHabitual at Gallery 825 in Los Angeles from February 8 to March 7, 2014.[2] Following his death in 2014, posthumous exhibitions honored his legacy through the Davyd Whaley Foundation. In 2016, the Foundation organized a pop-up exhibition of his works at Samuel Freeman Gallery in Los Angeles to launch its grant program, reintroducing his abstract oeuvre to the local scene.[14] The Foundation's third anniversary exhibition in 2019 at Castelli Art Space in Los Angeles, titled Three Years: The Davyd Whaley Foundation, included selections from Whaley's own collection alongside grant recipients' pieces, celebrating his enduring influence on emerging artists.[7] Media and Recognition Davyd Whaley's paintings gained visibility through placements in several popular television series directed by his husband, Norman Buckley, including Gossip Girl, The O.C., 90210, Melrose Place, The Carrie Diaries, The Client List, Switched at Birth, and Pretty Little Liars.[3] Several of his works were specifically featured in upcoming episodes of Pretty Little Liars during his lifetime.[8]Whaley's art also appeared at high-profile Hollywood events, such as the 2012 "Gallery for the People" exhibition hosted by producer Harvey Weinstein in Beverly Hills, which benefited The Art of Elysium and drew attendees including actresses Emma Roberts and Erika Christensen.[2][13]Whaley received coverage in art publications, including a feature interview in Design and Art Magazine in March 2013 that explored his journey from engineering to painting, and a 10-question profile in February 2014 highlighting his solo exhibition Subconscious Tendencies.[3][10]As an emerging artist in the Los Angeles scene, Whaley was recognized for his abstract, spiritually infused works and served as a resident artist at the Santa Fe Art Colony in downtown Los Angeles, where he maintained a large studio for full-time painting.[8][2] His contributions to public arts education earned him the 2012 Los Angeles Volunteer of the Year award from the Los Angeles City Council.[10] Legacy The Davyd Whaley Foundation The Davyd Whaley Foundation was established in 2016 by Norman Buckley, the husband of the late artist Davyd Whaley, to honor his lifelong philanthropy and dedication to service through the arts.[4][15]The foundation's mission centers on providing grants and scholarships to Los Angeles-area artists, with a particular emphasis on emerging and underserved individuals whose paths echo Whaley's own experiences as a self-taught painter committed to community and creative growth.[4][16][17]Among its key activities, the foundation has awarded annual grants since 2017, including the $10,000 Mid-Career Artist Grant to Margaret Griffith in 2017 and the Mid-Career Artist Grant to Phung Huynh in 2018, enabling recipients to advance their studio practices and exhibitions.[4][18][19] Later awards included the 2020 Artist-Teacher Grant to Rema Ghuloum and the 2022 Davyd Whaley Memorial Scholarship to Judy Giera at the Art Students League of New York.[20][21] It has also funded pop-up exhibitions featuring Whaley's work and that of grant recipients, such as the 2016 launch event at Samuel Freeman Gallery and the 2019 "Three Years" group show at Castelli Art Space, which highlighted seven supported artists including Laura Krifka and Lorenzo Hurtado Segovia.[14][22] These efforts have sustained support for visual artists in the region through ongoing programming as of 2025, including continued subsidization of scholarships at the Art Students League.[4][23] Monograph and Publications In 2016, a comprehensive monograph titled Davyd Whaley was published by The Davyd Whaley Foundation, serving as the primary documented overview of the artist's life, career, and oeuvre. The volume features a substantial essay titled "A Hero's Journey" by Peter Clothier, which explores Whaley's personal and professional trajectory, framing his artistic development as a spiritual quest marked by transformation and self-discovery.[1] Clothier's analysis delves into Whaley's artistic philosophy, emphasizing how his abstract paintings embodied a quest for inner meaning and psychological depth.[24]The monograph chronicles Whaley's transition from a career in engineering to abstract painting, highlighting pivotal moments such as his residency at the Santa Fe Art Colony and his embrace of intuitive creative processes.[24] It includes high-quality reproductions of key works from across his career, such as vibrant, layered abstractions that evoke archetypal forms and emotional resonance.[1] Particular attention is given to the Jungian elements in Whaley's art, with Clothier examining how motifs of the subconscious—drawn from Carl Jung's theories on the collective unconscious—manifest in Whaley's use of color, form, and symbolic layering to represent inner psychological landscapes.[25] This analysis underscores Whaley's paintings as visual explorations of synchronicity and the hero's archetypal journey, aligning his practice with broader themes of personal alchemy and universal human experience.[26]Edited by Norman Buckley, who also contributed a foreword, the publication plays a crucial role in preserving Whaley's legacy by compiling biographical details, artistic statements, and critical insights into a single, accessible volume spanning 200 pages.[27] Distributed through specialized art channels including independent bookstores like Book Soup and online platforms such as Amazon, the monograph has facilitated broader appreciation of Whaley's contributions, with proceeds supporting ongoing artistic initiatives.[24] The Foundation briefly promoted the book through events and its website, ensuring its reach within the Los Angeles art community.[4]
